<div class="thumbcaption">
<p>2016 World [civil] power generation by source [IEA, 2018] (Percentages of 24.973 TWh)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA-Report-keyworld-2018_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-Report-keyworld-2018-2">[2]</a></sup>

<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #313c42; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Coal (38.4%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ef8e39; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Natural Gas (23.2%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; </div>
</div></div>
<p><b>Wind power</b> is the use of <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">air flow</a> through <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> to provide the <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_power" title="Mechanical power">mechanical power</a> to turn <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">electric generators</a>. Wind power, as an alternative to burning <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>, is plentiful, <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable</a>, widely distributed, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">clean</a>, produces no <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions during operation, saves or conserves water, and uses little land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landuse_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-landuse-3">[3]</a></sup> The net <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_wind_power" title="Environmental impact of wind power">effects on the environment</a> are far less problematic than those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonrenewable_power" title="Nonrenewable power">nonrenewable power</a> sources.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">Wind farms</a> consist of many individual wind turbines, which are connected to the <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">electric power transmission</a> network. Onshore wind is an inexpensive source of electric power, competitive with or in many places cheaper than coal or gas plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Offshore wind is steadier and stronger than on land and <a href="/wiki/Offshore_wind_power" title="Offshore wind power">offshore farms</a> have less visual impact, but construction and maintenance costs are considerably higher. Small onshore wind farms can feed some energy into the grid or provide electric power to isolated off-grid locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wind_Energy_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wind_Energy-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wind power gives <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">variable power</a>, which is very consistent from year to year but has significant variation over shorter time scales. 
It is therefore used in conjunction with other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_power_source" title="Electric power source">electric power sources</a> to give a reliable supply. 
As the proportion of wind power in a region increases, a need to upgrade the grid and a lowered ability to supplant conventional production can occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ieawind_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ieawind-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abbess_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-abbess-9">[9]</a></sup> 
Power-management techniques such as having excess capacity, geographically distributed turbines, <a href="/wiki/Dispatchable_generation" title="Dispatchable generation">dispatchable</a> sources, sufficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_power" title="Hydroelectric power">hydroelectric power</a>, exporting and importing power to neighboring areas, or reducing demand when wind production is low, can in many cases overcome these problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eirgrid_impact_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-eirgrid_impact-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nicola_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-nicola-11">[11]</a></sup>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weather_forecast" title="Weather forecast">Weather forecasting</a> permits the electric-power network to be readied for the predictable variations in production that occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huang_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-huang-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, global wind power capacity expanded 10% to 539 GW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>. Yearly wind energy production grew 17% reaching 4.4% of worldwide electric power usage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bp2018_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-bp2018-16">[16]</a></sup> and providing 11.6% of the electricity in the European Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWEA2015_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-EWEA2015-17">[17]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> is the country with the highest penetration of wind power, with 43.4% of its consumed electricity from wind in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enet42_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-enet42-19">[19]</a></sup> 
At least 83 other countries around the world are using wind power to supply their electric power grids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren212011_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-ren212011-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_wind_power" title="History of wind power">History of wind power</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="739" data-file-width="714" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg/220px-Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg/330px-Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg/440px-Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charles_F._Brush" title="Charles F. Brush">Charles Brush's</a> windmill of 1888, used for generating electric power.</div></div></div>
<p>Wind power has been used as long as humans have put <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sailing_ships" title="Sailing ships">sails</a> into the wind. For more than two millennia <a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">wind-powered machines</a> have ground grain and pumped water. Wind power was widely available and not confined to the banks of fast-flowing streams, or later, requiring sources of fuel. Wind-powered pumps drained the <a href="/wiki/Polder#Polders_and_the_Netherlands" title="Polder">polders of the Netherlands</a>, and in arid regions such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_mid-west" title="American mid-west">American mid-west</a> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_outback" title="Australian outback">Australian outback</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_pump" title="Wind pump">wind pumps</a> provided water for livestock and steam engines.
</p><p>The first windmill used for the production of electric power was built in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> in July 1887 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prof_James_Blyth" title="Prof James Blyth">Prof James Blyth</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anderson%27s_College" title="Anderson's College">Anderson's College</a>, Glasgow (the precursor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strathclyde_University" title="Strathclyde University">Strathclyde University</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price-21">[21]</a></sup> Blyth's 10 metres (33 ft) high, cloth-sailed wind turbine was installed in the garden of his holiday cottage at <a href="/wiki/Marykirk" title="Marykirk">Marykirk</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kincardineshire" title="Kincardineshire">Kincardineshire</a> and was used to charge <a href="/wiki/Accumulator_(energy)" title="Accumulator (energy)">accumulators</a> developed by the Frenchman <a href="/wiki/Camille_Alphonse_Faure" title="Camille Alphonse Faure">Camille Alphonse Faure</a>, to power the lighting in the cottage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Price-21">[21]</a></sup> thus making it the first house in the world to have its electric power supplied by wind power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shackleton_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shackleton-22">[22]</a></sup> Blyth offered the surplus electric power to the people of Marykirk for lighting the main street, however, they turned down the offer as they thought electric power was "the work of the devil."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Price-21">[21]</a></sup> Although he later built a wind turbine to supply emergency power to the local Lunatic Asylum, Infirmary and Dispensary of <a href="/wiki/Montrose,_Angus" title="Montrose, Angus">Montrose</a> the invention never really caught on as the technology was not considered to be economically viable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Price-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Across the Atlantic, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleveland,_Ohio" title="Cleveland, Ohio">Cleveland, Ohio</a> a larger and heavily engineered machine was designed and constructed in the winter of 1887–1888 by <a href="/wiki/Charles_F._Brush" title="Charles F. Brush">Charles F. Brush</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> this was built by his engineering company at his home and operated from 1886 until 1900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The Brush wind turbine had a rotor 17 metres (56 ft) in diameter and was mounted on an 18 metres (59 ft) tower. Although large by today's standards, the machine was only rated at 12 kW. The connected dynamo was used either to charge a bank of batteries or to operate up to 100 <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">incandescent light bulbs</a>, three arc lamps, and various motors in Brush's laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the development of electric power, wind power found new applications in lighting buildings remote from centrally-generated power. Throughout the 20th century parallel paths developed small wind stations suitable for farms or residences, and larger utility-scale wind generators that could be connected to electric power grids for remote use of power. Today wind powered generators operate in every size range between tiny stations for battery charging at isolated residences, up to near-gigawatt sized <a href="/wiki/List_of_offshore_wind_farms" title="List of offshore wind farms">offshore wind farms</a> that provide electric power to national electrical networks.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind_energy">Wind energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Wind energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Map_of_Wind_Speed.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1490" data-file-width="2500" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Global_Map_of_Wind_Speed.png/220px-Global_Map_of_Wind_Speed.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Global_Map_of_Wind_Speed.png/330px-Global_Map_of_Wind_Speed.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Global_Map_of_Wind_Speed.png/440px-Global_Map_of_Wind_Speed.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_Map_of_Wind_Speed.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global map of wind speed at 100 m above surface level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-global_wind_atlas_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-global_wind_atlas-26">[26]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philippines_Wind_Power_Density_Map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2398" data-file-width="3390" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Philippines_Wind_Power_Density_Map.jpg/220px-Philippines_Wind_Power_Density_Map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Philippines_Wind_Power_Density_Map.jpg/330px-Philippines_Wind_Power_Density_Map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Philippines_Wind_Power_Density_Map.jpg/440px-Philippines_Wind_Power_Density_Map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philippines_Wind_Power_Density_Map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Philippines wind power density map at 100 m above surface level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-global_wind_atlas_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-global_wind_atlas-26">[26]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="340" data-file-width="600" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg/220px-Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg/330px-Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg/440px-Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distribution of wind speed (red) and energy (blue) for all of 2002 at the Lee Ranch facility in Colorado. The histogram shows measured data, while the curve is the Rayleigh model distribution for the same average wind speed.</div></div></div>
<p>Wind energy is the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of air in motion, also called <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a>.
Total wind energy flowing through an imaginary surface with area <i>A</i> during the time <i>t</i> is:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle E={\frac {1}{2}}mv^{2}={\frac {1}{2}}(Avt\rho )v^{2}={\frac {1}{2}}At\rho v^{3},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle E={\frac {1}{2}}mv^{2}={\frac {1}{2}}(Avt\rho )v^{2}={\frac {1}{2}}At\rho v^{3},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="E={\frac {1}{2}}mv^{2}={\frac {1}{2}}(Avt\rho )v^{2}={\frac {1}{2}}At\rho v^{3}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/768a83c369a7213461cfe88536d02e52d8a1a850" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:36.806ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physics_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-physics-27">[27]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>where <i>ρ</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Density_of_air" title="Density of air">density of air</a>; <i>v</i> is the wind <a href="/wiki/Speed" title="Speed">speed</a>; <i>Avt</i> is the volume of air passing through <i>A</i> (which is considered perpendicular to the direction of the wind); <i>Avtρ</i> is therefore the mass <i>m</i> passing through "A". Note that ½ <i>ρv</i><sup>2</sup> is the kinetic energy of the moving air per unit volume.
</p><p>Power is energy per unit time, so the wind power incident on <i>A</i> (e.g. equal to the rotor area of a wind turbine) is:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P={\frac {E}{t}}={\frac {1}{2}}A\rho v^{3}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>E</mi>
<mi>t</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mi>A</mi>
<mi>ρ<!-- ρ --></mi>
<msup>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P={\frac {E}{t}}={\frac {1}{2}}A\rho v^{3}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P={\frac {E}{t}}={\frac {1}{2}}A\rho v^{3}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/30739560fad0d0b3435acd7a9acdc0ae045e7306" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:18.326ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physics_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-physics-27">[27]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Wind power in an open air stream is thus <i>proportional</i> to the <i>third power</i> of the wind speed; the available power increases eightfold when the wind speed doubles. Wind turbines for grid electric power therefore need to be especially efficient at greater wind speeds.
</p><p>Wind is the movement of air across the surface of the Earth, affected by areas of high pressure and of low pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-what_is_wind?_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-what_is_wind?-28">[28]</a></sup>
The global wind kinetic energy averaged approximately 1.50 MJ/m<sup>2</sup> over the period from 1979 to 2010, 1.31 MJ/m<sup>2</sup> in the Northern Hemisphere with 1.70 MJ/m<sup>2</sup> in the Southern Hemisphere. The atmosphere acts as a thermal engine, absorbing heat at higher temperatures, releasing heat at lower temperatures. The process is responsible for production of wind kinetic energy at a rate of 2.46 W/m<sup>2</sup> sustaining thus the circulation of the atmosphere against frictional dissipation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Through <a href="/wiki/Wind_resource_assessment" title="Wind resource assessment">wind resource assessment</a> it is possible to provide estimates of wind power potential globally, by country or region, or for a specific site. A global assessment of wind power potential is available via the Global Wind Atlas provided by the <a href="/wiki/Technical_University_of_Denmark" title="Technical University of Denmark">Technical University of Denmark</a> in partnership with the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-global_wind_atlas_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-global_wind_atlas-26">[26]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
Unlike 'static' wind resource atlases which average estimates of wind speed and power density across multiple years, tools such as <a href="/wiki/Renewables.ninja" title="Renewables.ninja">Renewables.ninja</a> provide time-varying simulations of wind speed and power output from different wind turbine models at an hourly resolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>. More detailed, site specific assessments of wind resource potential can be obtained from specialist commercial providers, and many of the larger wind developers will maintain in-house modeling capabilities.
</p><p>The total amount of economically extractable power available from the wind is considerably more than present human power use from all sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> 
Axel Kleidon of the <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck_Society" title="Max Planck Society">Max Planck Institute</a> in Germany, carried out a "top down" calculation on how much wind energy there is, starting with the incoming solar radiation that drives the winds by creating temperature differences in the atmosphere. He concluded that somewhere between 18 TW and 68 TW could be extracted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsc2012_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsc2012-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cristina Archer and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Z._Jacobson" title="Mark Z. Jacobson">Mark Z. Jacobson</a> presented a "bottom-up" estimate, which unlike Kleidon's are based on actual measurements of wind speeds, and found that there is 1700 TW of wind power at an altitude of 100 metres over land and sea. Of this, "between 72 and 170 TW could be extracted in a practical and cost-competitive manner".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsc2012_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-nsc2012-34">[34]</a></sup> They later estimated 80 TW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson2012_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobson2012-35">[35]</a></sup> However research at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> estimates 1 Watt/m<sup>2</sup> on average and 2–10 MW/km<sup>2</sup> capacity for large scale wind farms, suggesting that these estimates of total global wind resources are too high by a factor of about 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The strength of wind varies, and an average value for a given location does not alone indicate the amount of energy a wind turbine could produce there.
</p><p>To assess prospective wind power sites a probability distribution function is often fit to the observed wind speed data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Different locations will have different wind speed distributions. The <a href="/wiki/Weibull_distribution" title="Weibull distribution">Weibull</a> model closely mirrors the actual distribution of hourly/ten-minute wind speeds at many locations. The Weibull factor is often close to 2 and therefore a <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_distribution" title="Rayleigh distribution">Rayleigh distribution</a> can be used as a less accurate, but simpler model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>A wind farm is a group of <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> in the same location used for production of electric power. A large wind farm may consist of several hundred individual wind turbines distributed over an extended area, but the land between the turbines may be used for agricultural or other purposes. For example, <a href="/wiki/Gansu_Wind_Farm" title="Gansu Wind Farm">Gansu Wind Farm</a>, the largest wind farm in the world, has several thousand turbines. A wind farm may also be located offshore.
</p><p>Almost all large wind turbines have the same design — a horizontal axis wind turbine having an upwind rotor with three blades, attached to a nacelle on top of a tall tubular tower.
</p><p>In a wind farm, individual turbines are interconnected with a medium voltage (often 34.5 kV), power collection system and communications network. In general, a distance of 7D (7 × Rotor Diameter of the Wind Turbine) is set between each turbine in a fully developed wind farm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> At a substation, this medium-voltage electric current is increased in voltage with a <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">transformer</a> for connection to the high voltage <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">electric power transmission</a> system.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Generator_characteristics_and_stability">Generator characteristics and stability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Generator characteristics and stability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Induction_generator" title="Induction generator">Induction generators</a>, which were often used for wind power projects in the 1980s and 1990s, require <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactive_power" title="Reactive power">reactive power</a> for <a href="/wiki/Excitation_(magnetic)" title="Excitation (magnetic)">excitation</a> so <a href="/wiki/Electrical_substation" title="Electrical substation">substations</a> used in wind-power collection systems include substantial <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">capacitor</a> banks for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_factor_correction" title="Power factor correction">power factor correction</a>. Different types of wind turbine generators behave differently during transmission grid disturbances, so <a href="/wiki/Wind_energy_software" title="Wind energy software">extensive modelling</a> of the dynamic electromechanical characteristics of a new wind farm is required by transmission system operators to ensure predictable stable behaviour during system faults. In particular, induction generators cannot support the system voltage during faults, unlike steam or hydro turbine-driven synchronous generators.
</p><p>Today these generators aren't used any more in modern turbines. Instead today most turbines use variable speed generators combined with partial- or full-scale power converter between the turbine generator and the collector system, which generally have more desirable properties for grid interconnection and have <a href="/wiki/Low_voltage_ride_through" title="Low voltage ride through">Low voltage ride through</a>-capabilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Modern concepts use either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doubly_fed_electric_machine" title="Doubly fed electric machine">doubly fed machines</a> with partial-scale converters or squirrel-cage induction generators or synchronous generators (both permanently and electrically excited) with full scale converters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Transmission systems operators will supply a wind farm developer with a <a href="/wiki/Grid_code" title="Grid code">grid code</a> to specify the requirements for interconnection to the transmission grid. This will include <a href="/wiki/Power_factor" title="Power factor">power factor</a>, constancy of <a href="/wiki/Utility_frequency" title="Utility frequency">frequency</a> and dynamic behaviour of the wind farm turbines during a system fault.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demeo2005_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Demeo2005-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavadil2005_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zavadil2005-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Offshore_wind_power">Offshore wind power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Offshore wind power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2739" data-file-width="3651" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg/220px-Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg/330px-Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg/440px-Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The world's second full-scale <a href="/wiki/Floating_wind_turbine" title="Floating wind turbine">floating wind turbine</a> (and first to be installed without the use of heavy-lift vessels), WindFloat, operating at rated capacity (2 MW) approximately 5 km offshore of <a href="/wiki/P%C3%B3voa_de_Varzim" title="Póvoa de Varzim">Póvoa de Varzim</a>, Portugal</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Offshore_wind_power" title="Offshore wind power">Offshore wind power</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_offshore_wind_farms" title="List of offshore wind farms">List of offshore wind farms</a></div>
<p>Offshore wind power refers to the construction of wind farms in large bodies of water to generate electric power. These installations can utilize the more frequent and powerful winds that are available in these locations and have less aesthetic impact on the landscape than land based projects. However, the construction and the maintenance costs are considerably higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hulazan_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hulazan-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Millborrow_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Millborrow-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vestas" title="Vestas">Vestas</a> are the leading turbine suppliers for offshore wind power. <a href="/wiki/%C3%98rsted_(company)" title="Ørsted (company)">Ørsted</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vattenfall" title="Vattenfall">Vattenfall</a> and <a href="/wiki/E.ON" title="E.ON">E.ON</a> are the leading offshore operators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-btm2010o_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-btm2010o-57">[57]</a></sup> As of October 2010, 3.16 GW of offshore wind power capacity was operational, mainly in Northern Europe. According to <a href="/wiki/BTM_Consult" title="BTM Consult">BTM Consult</a>, more than 16 GW of additional capacity will be installed before the end of 2014 and the UK and Germany will become the two leading markets. Offshore wind power capacity is expected to reach a total of 75 GW worldwide by 2020, with significant contributions from China and the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-btm2010o_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-btm2010o-57">[57]</a></sup> The UK's investments in offshore wind power have resulted in a rapid decrease of the usage of coal as an energy source between 2012 and 2017, as well as a drop in the usage of natural gas as an energy source in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, 1,662 turbines at 55 offshore wind farms in 10 European countries produced 18 TWh, enough to power almost five million households.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> As of September 2018 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walney_Extension" title="Walney Extension">Walney Extension</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> is the largest offshore wind farm in the world at 659 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">MW</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-walney_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-walney-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>

<tr>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collection_and_transmission_network">Collection and transmission network</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Collection and transmission network">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farm</a>, individual turbines are interconnected with a medium voltage (usually 34.5 kV) power collection system and communications network. At a substation, this medium-voltage electric current is increased in voltage with a transformer for connection to the high voltage <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">electric power transmission</a> system.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:250px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Vetropark_Ko%C5%A1ava_Zagajica.ogv/854px--Vetropark_Ko%C5%A1ava_Zagajica.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="21.021333333333" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Vetropark_Košava_Zagajica.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/0/0f/Vetropark_Ko%C5%A1ava_Zagajica.ogv/Vetropark_Ko%C5%A1ava_Zagajica.ogv.480p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="284" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="203152" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/0/0f/Vetropark_Ko%C5%A1ava_Zagajica.ogv/Vetropark_Ko%C5%A1ava_Zagajica.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="580616" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'><img alt="File:Vetropark Košava Zagajica.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Vetropark_Ko%C5%A1ava_Zagajica.ogv/250px--Vetropark_Ko%C5%A1ava_Zagajica.ogv.jpg" style="width:250px;height:141px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0f/Vetropark_Ko%C5%A1ava_Zagajica.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vetropark_Ko%C5%A1ava_Zagajica.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wind Power in <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a></div></div></div>
<p>A transmission line is required to bring the generated power to (often remote) markets. For an off-shore station this may require a submarine cable. Construction of a new high-voltage line may be too costly for the wind resource alone, but wind sites may take advantage of lines installed for conventionally fueled generation.
</p><p>One of the biggest current challenges to wind power grid integration in the United States is the necessity of developing new transmission lines to carry power from wind farms, usually in remote lowly populated states in the middle of the country due to availability of wind, to high load locations, usually on the coasts where population density is higher. The current transmission lines in remote locations were not designed for the transport of large amounts of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-69">[69]</a></sup> As transmission lines become longer the losses associated with power transmission increase, as modes of losses at lower lengths are exacerbated and new modes of losses are no longer negligible as the length is increased, making it harder to transport large loads over large distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> However, resistance from state and local governments makes it difficult to construct new transmission lines. Multi state power transmission projects are discouraged by states with cheap electric power rates for fear that exporting their cheap power will lead to increased rates. A 2005 energy law gave the Energy Department authority to approve transmission projects states refused to act on, but after an attempt to use this authority, the Senate declared the department was being overly aggressive in doing so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-69">[69]</a></sup> Another problem is that wind companies find out after the fact that the transmission capacity of a new farm is below the generation capacity, largely because federal utility rules to encourage renewable energy installation allow feeder lines to meet only minimum standards. These are important issues that need to be solved, as when the transmission capacity does not meet the generation capacity, wind farms are forced to produce below their full potential or stop running all together, in a process known as curtailment. While this leads to potential renewable generation left untapped, it prevents possible grid overload or risk to reliable service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind_power_capacity_and_production">Wind power capacity and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Wind power capacity and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_by_country" title="Wind power by country">Wind power by country</a></div>

<div style="position:absolute;left:142.5px;height:10px;width:1px;border-left:1px solid black;"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:200px;top:10px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;text-align:center;vertical-align:top;">2010</div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:202.5px;height:10px;width:1px;border-left:1px solid black;"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:260px;top:10px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;text-align:center;vertical-align:top;">2014</div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:262.5px;height:10px;width:1px;border-left:1px solid black;"></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div></div><div class="thumbcaption">Global annual new installed wind capacity 1997–2015 (in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">MW</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GWEC-2014_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC-2014-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>3</span></sup></div></div></div>
<p>As of 2015, there are over 200,000 wind turbines operating, with a total <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a> of 432 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">GW</a> worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Globe_and_Mail_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Globe_and_Mail-74">[74]</a></sup> 
The <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> passed 100 GW nameplate capacity in September 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> while the United States surpassed 75 GW in 2015 and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Wind power in the People's Republic of China">China</a>'s grid connected capacity passed 145 GW in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Globe_and_Mail_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Globe_and_Mail-74">[74]</a></sup>
In 2015 wind power constituted 15.6% of all installed power generation capacity in the European Union and it generated around 11.4% of its power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWEA2015_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-EWEA2015-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>World wind generation capacity more than quadrupled between 2000 and 2006, doubling about every 3 years. 
<a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_States" title="Wind power in the United States">The United States pioneered wind farms</a> and led the world in installed capacity in the 1980s and into the 1990s. 
In 1997 installed capacity in Germany surpassed the United States and led until once again overtaken by the United States in 2008. 
China has been rapidly expanding its wind installations in the late 2000s and passed the United States in 2010 to become the world leader. 
As of 2011, 83 countries around the world were using wind power on a commercial basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren212011_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-ren212011-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The actual amount of electric power that wind is able to generate is calculated by multiplying the <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factor</a>, which varies according to equipment and location. 
Estimates of the capacity factors for wind installations are in the range of 35% to 44%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>

</div>
</div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_trends">Growth trends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Growth trends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png/220px-GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png/330px-GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png/440px-GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Worldwide installed wind power capacity forecast<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GWEC_Market_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC_Market-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GWEC_Forcast_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC_Forcast-78">[78]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The wind power industry set new records in 2014 – more than 50 GW of new capacity was installed. Another record breaking year occurred in 2015, with 22% annual market growth resulting in the 60 GW mark being passed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GWEC-Forecast-2016_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC-Forecast-2016-79">[79]</a></sup> In 2015, close to half of all new wind power was added outside of the traditional markets in Europe and North America. This was largely from new construction in China and India. <a href="/wiki/Global_Wind_Energy_Council" title="Global Wind Energy Council">Global Wind Energy Council</a> (GWEC) figures show that 2015 recorded an increase of installed capacity of more than 63 GW, taking the total installed wind energy capacity to 432.9 GW, up from 74 GW in 2006. In terms of economic value, the wind energy sector has become one of the important players in the energy markets, with the total investments reaching <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>329bn (<a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">€</a>296.6bn), an increase of 4% over 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GWEC-2015_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC-2015-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwec2007_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-gwec2007-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_industry" title="Wind power industry">wind power industry</a> was affected by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late-2000s_recession" title="Late-2000s recession">global financial crisis</a> in 2009 and 2010, GWEC predicts that the installed capacity of wind power will be 792.1 GW by the end of 2020<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GWEC-Forecast-2016_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC-Forecast-2016-79">[79]</a></sup> and 4,042 GW by end of 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GWEC-Forecast-2014_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC-Forecast-2014-82">[82]</a></sup> The increased commissioning of wind power is being accompanied by record low prices for forthcoming renewable electric power. In some cases, wind onshore is already the cheapest electric power generation option and costs are continuing to decline. The contracted prices for wind onshore for the next few years are now as low as 30 USD/MWh.
</p><p>In the EU in 2015, 44% of all new generating capacity was wind power; while in the same period net fossil fuel power capacity decreased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWEA2015_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-EWEA2015-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capacity_factor">Capacity factor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Capacity factor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since wind speed is not constant, a wind farm's annual <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> production is never as much as the sum of the generator nameplate ratings multiplied by the total hours in a year. The ratio of actual productivity in a year to this theoretical maximum is called the <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factor</a>. Typical capacity factors are 15–50%; values at the upper end of the range are achieved in favourable sites and are due to wind turbine design improvements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ceereCapInter_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-ceereCapInter-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-capacity-factor-50_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-capacity-factor-50-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[nb 1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Online data is available for some locations, and the capacity factor can be calculated from the yearly output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MassMaritime_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-MassMaritime-86">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iesoOntarioWind_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-iesoOntarioWind-87">[86]</a></sup> For example, the German nationwide average wind power capacity factor over all of 2012 was just under 17.5% (45,867 GW·h/yr / (29.9 GW × 24 × 366) = 0.1746),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> and the capacity factor for Scottish wind farms averaged 24% between 2008 and 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WindPower_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-WindPower-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike fueled generating plants, the capacity factor is affected by several parameters, including the variability of the wind at the site and the size of the <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">generator</a> relative to the turbine's swept area. A small generator would be cheaper and achieve a higher capacity factor but would produce less <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electric power</a> (and thus less profit) in high winds. Conversely, a large generator would cost more but generate little extra power and, depending on the type, may <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stall_(flight)" title="Stall (flight)">stall</a> out at low wind speed. Thus an optimum capacity factor of around 40–50% would be aimed for.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-capacity-factor-50_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-capacity-factor-50-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-capFactors_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-capFactors-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2008 study released by the U.S. Department of Energy noted that the capacity factor of new wind installations was increasing as the technology improves, and projected further improvements for future capacity factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Windpowering_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Windpowering-91">[90]</a></sup> In 2010, the department estimated the capacity factor of new wind turbines in 2010 to be 45%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> The annual average capacity factor for wind generation in the US has varied between 29.8% and 34% during the period 2010–2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Penetration">Penetration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Penetration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable floatright">

</td></tr>

<td>8%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_States" title="Wind power in the United States">United States</a> (2016)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CT_2016_USA_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-CT_2016_USA-101">[100]</a></sup>
</td>
<td>5.5%
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Wind energy penetration is the fraction of energy produced by wind compared with the total generation. The wind power penetration in world electric power generation in 2015 was 3.5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is no generally accepted maximum level of wind penetration. The limit for a particular <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">grid</a> will depend on the existing generating plants, pricing mechanisms, capacity for <a href="/wiki/Energy_storage" title="Energy storage">energy storage</a>, demand management and other factors. An interconnected electric power grid will already include <a href="/wiki/Operating_reserve" title="Operating reserve">reserve generating</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission#Capacity" title="Electric power transmission">transmission capacity</a> to allow for equipment failures. This reserve capacity can also serve to compensate for the varying power generation produced by wind stations. Studies have indicated that 20% of the total annual electrical energy consumption may be incorporated with minimal difficulty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tacklingUS_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-tacklingUS-104">[103]</a></sup> These studies have been for locations with geographically dispersed wind farms, some degree of <a href="/wiki/Dispatchable_generation" title="Dispatchable generation">dispatchable energy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a> with storage capacity, demand management, and interconnected to a large grid area enabling the export of electric power when needed. Beyond the 20% level, there are few technical limits, but the economic implications become more significant. Electrical utilities continue to study the effects of large scale penetration of wind generation on system stability and economics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGestimates_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-NGestimates-105">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minnesota_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-minnesota-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESB2004Study_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESB2004Study-107">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sinclairMerz_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-sinclairMerz-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>A wind energy penetration figure can be specified for different duration of time, but is often quoted annually. To obtain 100% from wind annually requires substantial long term storage or substantial interconnection to other systems which may already have substantial storage. On a monthly, weekly, daily, or hourly basis—or less—wind might supply as much as or more than 100% of current use, with the rest stored or exported. Seasonal industry might then take advantage of high wind and low usage times such as at night when wind output can exceed normal demand. Such industry might include production of silicon, aluminum,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> steel, or of natural gas, and hydrogen, and using future long term storage to facilitate 100% energy from <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">variable renewable energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> Homes can also be programmed to accept extra electric power on demand, for example by remotely turning up water heater thermostats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Australia, the state of South Australia generates around half of the nation's wind power capacity. By the end of 2011 wind power in South Australia, championed by Premier (and Climate Change Minister) Mike Rann, reached 26% of the State's electric power generation, edging out coal for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Policy_2012_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-National_Policy_2012-113">[112]</a></sup> At this stage South Australia, with only 7.2% of Australia's population, had 54% of Australia's installed capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ree2011prelim_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-ree2011prelim-97">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Policy_2012_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-National_Policy_2012-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variability">Variability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Variability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">Variable renewable energy</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Grid_balancing" title="Grid balancing">Grid balancing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toro_de_osborne.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2538" data-file-width="3872" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Toro_de_osborne.jpg/220px-Toro_de_osborne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Toro_de_osborne.jpg/330px-Toro_de_osborne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Toro_de_osborne.jpg/440px-Toro_de_osborne.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Toro_de_osborne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wind turbines are typically installed in favorable windy locations. In the image, wind power <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Spain" title="Wind power in Spain">generators in Spain</a>, near an <a href="/wiki/Osborne_bull" title="Osborne bull">Osborne bull</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Electric power generated from wind power can be highly variable at several different timescales: hourly, daily, or seasonally. Annual variation also exists, but is not as significant. Because instantaneous electrical generation and consumption must remain in balance to maintain grid stability, this variability can present substantial challenges to incorporating large amounts of wind power into a grid system. Intermittency and the non-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermittent_power_sources#Terminology" title="Intermittent power sources">dispatchable</a> nature of wind energy production can raise costs for regulation, incremental <a href="/wiki/Operating_reserve" title="Operating reserve">operating reserve</a>, and (at high penetration levels) could require an increase in the already existing <a href="/wiki/Energy_demand_management" title="Energy demand management">energy demand management</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Load_shedding" title="Load shedding">load shedding</a>, storage solutions or system interconnection with <a href="/wiki/High-voltage_direct_current" title="High-voltage direct current">HVDC</a> cables.
</p><p>Fluctuations in load and allowance for failure of large fossil-fuel generating units requires operating reserve capacity, which can be increased to compensate for variability of wind generation.
</p><p>Wind power is variable, and during low wind periods it must be replaced by other power sources. Transmission networks presently cope with outages of other generation plants and daily changes in electrical demand, but the variability of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermittent_power_source" title="Intermittent power source">intermittent power sources</a> such as wind power, is more frequent than those of conventional power generation plants which, when scheduled to be operating, may be able to deliver their nameplate capacity around 95% of the time.
</p><p>Presently, grid systems with large wind penetration require a small increase in the frequency of usage of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> spinning reserve power plants to prevent a loss of electric power in the event that there is no wind. At low wind power penetration, this is less of an issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-is_windpower_reliable_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-is_windpower_reliable-114">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clavertonReliable_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-clavertonReliable-115">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>GE has installed a prototype wind turbine with onboard battery similar to that of an electric car, equivalent of 1 minute of production. Despite the small capacity, it is enough to guarantee that power output complies with forecast for 15 minutes, as the battery is used to eliminate the difference rather than provide full output. In certain cases the increased predictability can be used to take wind power penetration from 20 to 30 or 40 per cent. The battery cost can be retrieved by selling burst power on demand and reducing backup needs from gas plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the UK there were 124 separate occasions from 2008 to 2010 when the nation's wind output fell to less than 2% of installed capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> A report on Denmark's wind power noted that their wind power network provided less than 1% of average demand on 54 days during the year 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denmark_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denmark-119">[118]</a></sup> Wind power advocates argue that these periods of low wind can be dealt with by simply restarting existing power stations that have been held in readiness, or interlinking with HVDC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Czisch-Giebel_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Czisch-Giebel-120">[119]</a></sup> Electrical grids with slow-responding thermal power plants and without ties to networks with hydroelectric generation may have to limit the use of wind power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denmark_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Denmark-119">[118]</a></sup> According to a 2007 Stanford University study published in the <i>Journal of Applied Meteorology and Climatology</i>, interconnecting ten or more wind farms can allow an average of 33% of the total energy produced (i.e. about 8% of total nameplate capacity) to be used as reliable, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baseload_power" title="Baseload power">baseload electric power</a> which can be relied on to handle peak loads, as long as minimum criteria are met for wind speed and turbine height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-connecting_wind_farms_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-connecting_wind_farms-121">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Archer2007_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Archer2007-122">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>Conversely, on particularly windy days, even with penetration levels of 16%, wind power generation can surpass all other electric power sources in a country. In Spain, in the early hours of 16 April 2012 wind power production reached the highest percentage of electric power production till then, at 60.46% of the total demand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eolica_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-eolica-123">[122]</a></sup> In Denmark, which had power market penetration of 30% in 2013, over 90 hours, wind power generated 100% of the country's power, peaking at 122% of the country's demand at 2 am on 28 October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable floatright">
